                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Litsea genome and the evolution of the laurel family"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-04-03"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The laurel family within the Magnoliids has attracted attentions owing to its scents, variable inflorescences, and controversial phylogenetic position. Here, we present a chromosome-level assembly of the Litsea cubeba genome, together with low-coverage genomic and transcriptomic data for many other Lauraceae. Phylogenomic analyses show phylogenetic discordance at the position of Magnoliids, suggesting incomplete lineage sorting during the divergence of monocots, eudicots, and Magnoliids. An ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) event occurred just before the divergence of Laurales and Magnoliales; subsequently, independent WGDs occurred almost simultaneously in the three Lauralean lineages. The phylogenetic relationships within Lauraceae correspond to the divergence of inflorescences, as evidenced by the phylogeny of FUWA, a conserved gene involved in determining panicle architecture in Lauraceae. Monoterpene synthases responsible for production of specific volatile compounds in Lauraceae are functionally verified. Our work sheds light on the evolution of the Lauraceae, the genetic basis for floral evolution and specific scents. Litsea cubeba belongs to the Lauraceae family within the Magnoliids clade. Here, the authors assemble its genome and reveal divergence of inflorescence and sexual differentiation, the phylogenetic relationships across the Lauraceae and related species, and biosynthetic genes related to essential oil synthesis."/>

itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Shaoguan University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1790 3732, grid.412549.f, Henry Fok College of Biology and Agriculture, Shaoguan University, Shaoguan, 512005, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope"             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The laurel family within the Magnoliids has attracted attentions owing to its scents, variable inflorescences, and controversial phylogenetic position. Here, we present a chromosome-level assembly of the <i>Litsea cubeba</i> genome, together with low-coverage genomic and transcriptomic data for many other Lauraceae. Phylogenomic analyses show phylogenetic discordance at the position of Magnoliids, suggesting incomplete lineage sorting during the divergence of monocots, eudicots, and Magnoliids. An ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) event occurred just before the divergence of Laurales and Magnoliales; subsequently, independent WGDs occurred almost simultaneously in the three Lauralean lineages. The phylogenetic relationships within Lauraceae correspond to the divergence of inflorescences, as evidenced by the phylogeny of <i>FUWA</i>, a conserved gene involved in determining panicle architecture in Lauraceae. Monoterpene synthases responsible for production of specific volatile compounds in Lauraceae are functionally verified. Our work sheds light on the evolution of the Lauraceae, the genetic basis for floral evolution and specific scents.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Lauraceae, also referred to as the laurel family, is a family from the order Laurales in Magnoliids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Chase, M. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1–20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d65370e897">1</a></sup>. The family includes a total of 2500–3000 globally distributed species in 44 genera of the woody subfamily Lauroideae and about 25 species in 1 genus of the parasitic subfamily Cassythoideae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li, S. G. et al. Lauraceae. FOC 7, 102–153 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65370e901">2</a></sup>. The morphological features of flowers in Lauraceae species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Akagi, T. et al. Two Y-chromosome-encoded genes determine sex in kiwifruit. Nat. Plants 5, 801–809 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d65370e905">3</a></sup>, including various inflorescences and the existence of both bisexual and unisexual flowers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Endress, P. K. &amp; Lorence, D. H. Inflorescence structure in laurales—stable and flexible patterns. Int. J. Plant Sci. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1086/706449&#xA;&#xA; (2020)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d65370e909">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Van der Werff, H. Alseodaphnopsis (Lauraceae) revisited. Blumea 64, 186–189 (2019)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d65370e909_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Rohwer, J. G. The timing of nectar secretion in staminal and staminodial glands in Lauraceae. Plant Biol. 11, 490–492 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d65370e912">6</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">1</a>), provide a reference for studying flower evolution in angiosperms. In addition, the specific scents from Lauraceae species have made the laurel family economically important as a source of medicine, spices, and perfumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li, S. G. et al. Lauraceae. FOC 7, 102–153 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65370e920">2</a></sup>. A diverse array of terpenoids, mainly monoterpenes and sesquiterpenes, defines the scents of different species in Lauraceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kilic, A., Hafizoglu, H., Kollmannsberger, H. &amp; Nitz, S. Volatile constituents and key odorants in leaves, buds, flowers, and fruits of Laurus nobilis L. J. Agr. Food Chem. 52, 1601–1606 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d65370e924">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Singh, R. &amp; Jawaid, T. Cinnamomum camphora (Kapur): review. Pharmacogn. J. 4, 1–5 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d65370e927">8</a></sup>. Terpene synthases (TPSs) have been primarily responsible for the monoterpene production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 66, 212–229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d65370e931">9</a></sup>; however, research on the TPS gene families in Lauraceae is still in its infancy due to the hitherto limited genomic data. From a phylogenetic perspective, the relationships among Magnoliids, monocots, and eudicots still remain to be debated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Massoni, J., Couvreur, T. L. P. &amp; Sauquet, H. Five major shifts of diversification through the long evolutionary history of magnoliidae (angiosperms). BMC Evol. Biol. 15, 49 (2015)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d65370e935">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species-pair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18–25 (2018)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d65370e935_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e935_2">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Nuclear genomes of two Magnoliids. Nat. Plants 5, 6–7 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d65370e938">13</a></sup>. For instance, the analysis of the <i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e944">12</a></sup> genome supported a sister relationship of Magnoliids and eudicots to the exclusion of monocots, while the genomes of <i>Liriodendron chinense</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species-pair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18–25 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d65370e951">11</a></sup> and <i>Persea americana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Rendón-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65370e957">14</a></sup> suggested Magnoliids as a sister group to the clade consisting of both eudicots and monocots. Still some other studies, amongst those based on organellar genes and a limited number of nuclear genes, support a sister relationship between Magnoliids and monocots, to the exclusion of eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sun, M. et al. Deep phylogenetic incongruence in the angiosperm clade. Rosidae. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 83, 156–166 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d65370e961">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zhang, N., Zeng, L., Shan, H. &amp; Ma, H. Highly conserved low‐copy nuclear genes as effective markers for phylogenetic analyses in angiosperms. N. Phytol. 195, 923–937 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d65370e964">16</a></sup>. The often-conflicting evolutionary relationships also reflect the morphological complexities among monocots, eudicots, and Magnoliids. For example, the spiral floral phyllotaxis is present in Magnoliids and eudicots, but not in monocots; and Magnoliids and eudicots generally have carpels with one, two, or more ovules, while most monocots have more than two ovules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Endress, P. K. &amp; Doyle, J. A. Ancestral traits and specializations in the flowers of the basal grade of living angiosperms. Taxon 64, 1093–1116 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d65370e968">17</a></sup>. However, flowers are trimerous in Magnoliids and monocots but tetramerous or pentamerous in eudicots. Intermediate ascidiate carpels are predominantly present in Magnoliids, which differentiates them from other angiosperm lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Endress, P. K. &amp; Doyle, J. A. Ancestral traits and specializations in the flowers of the basal grade of living angiosperms. Taxon 64, 1093–1116 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d65370e972">17</a></sup>.</p><p>As two species, <i>C. kanehirae</i> and <i>P. americana</i>, from the core Lauraceae (including the Laureae-<i>Cinnamomeae</i> group and <i>Persea</i> group)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Rohwer, J. G. &amp; Rudolph, B. Jumping genera: the phylogenetic positions of Cassytha, Hypodaphnis, and Neocinnamomum (Lauraceae) based on different analyses of trnK intron sequences. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 92, 153–178 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d65370e991">18</a></sup> have already been sequenced<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e996">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Rendón-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65370e999">14</a></sup>, we here present a chromosome-level assembly of the genome of May Chang tree (<i>Litsea cubeba</i> Lour.), which is from the sister clade to <i>C. kanehirae</i> in the core Lauraceae. It is an important species for producing essential oils (roughly 95% terpenoid) that are widely used in perfumes, cosmetics, and medicine all over the world<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang, X. W., Feng, Y. C. &amp; Huang, Y. Potential cosmetic application of essential oil extracted from Litsea cubeba fruits from China. J. Essent. Oil Res. 25, 112–119 (2013)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1009">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Su, Y. C. &amp; Ho, C. L. Essential oil compositions and antimicrobial activities of various parts of Litsea cubeba from Taiwan. Nat. Prod. Commun. 11, 515–518 (2016)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1009_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Nguyen, H. V. et al. Litsea cubeba leaf essential oil from Vietnam: chemical diversity and its impacts on antibacterial activity. Lett. Appl. Microbiol. 66, 207–214 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1012">21</a></sup>. Further, to revisit the phylogenetic position of Magnoliids relative to eudicots and monocots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species-pair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18–25 (2018)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1016">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1016_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Nuclear genomes of two Magnoliids. Nat. Plants 5, 6–7 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1019">13</a></sup> and to study the evolutionary relationships within the Lauraceae, we sequence the genomes of 47 species of 20 genera in Lauraceae at a low coverage. Also, to uncover the molecular basis for the various floral features and the biosynthesis of scents in Lauraceae, we analyze mixed-tissue and flower bud transcriptomes for 23 species of 16 genera in the Lauraceae family, following by further functional verifications using transient overexpression and enzyme activity assay.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and annotation</h3><p><i>L. cubeba</i> has a diploid genome (2<i>n</i> = 24) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2a</a>) with an estimated haploid genome size of 1370.14 Mb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2b</a>). The genome was initially assembled with 155.64× sequencing reads from the PacBio platform. The assembled contigs were subsequently linked into 1514 scaffolds with 10× genomics barcoded reads (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">1</a>). We obtained an initial genome assembly with 1514 scaffolds covering 1325.69 Mb, with a contig N50 value of 607.34 kb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2</a>). Further scaffolding was done based on 292.17 Gb reads from a sequencing library of Genome-wide Chromosome Conformation Capture (Hi-C). We were able to anchor a total of 1018 scaffolds covering 1253.47 Mb (94.56%) of the assembled genome into 12 pseudochromosomes (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2</a>c, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">3</a>, and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">3</a>). To confirm the completeness of the assembly, we performed CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1065">22</a></sup>, BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1069">23</a></sup> assessments, and used mRNA sequences of <i>L. cubeba</i> and found the completeness of the genome to be 95.97% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">4</a>), 88.4% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">5</a>), and 97% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">6</a>), respectively. A combination of homolog-based comparisons and structure-based analyses resulted in an annotation of 735 Mb transposable elements (TEs), representing 55.47% of the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">7</a>), which is between that of <i>C. kanehirae</i> (~48% in a 730.7 Mb genome)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1096">12</a></sup> and <i>L. chinense</i> (~62% in a 1742.4 Mb genome)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species-pair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18–25 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1103">11</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">8</a>). Long terminal repeats (LTRs) are the predominant TEs in the genome of <i>L. cubeba</i>, which represent 47.64% (631 Mb) of the whole genome. Both <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>L. chinense</i> have a larger genome size than that of <i>C. kanehireae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1122">12</a></sup>, and they both contain higher content of LTR/gypsy and copia elements (45.31%) than that of the <i>C. kanehireae</i> genome (16.50%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1129">12</a></sup>. Hence, it suggests that LTR/gypsy and copia elements contribute most to the expansions of the <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>L. chinense</i> genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><p>A high-confidence set of 31,329 protein-coding genes were predicted in the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome, of which 29,262 (93.4%) and 27,753 (88.59%) were supported by transcriptome data and protein homologs, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">4a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a>). A total of 29,651 (94.6%) predicted protein-coding genes were functionally annotated (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">4a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">10</a>) and 30,314 (91.3%) of the genes could be located on the 12 pseudochromosomes. In addition, 1284 (89.2%) of the 1440 protein-coding genes in the BUSCO plant set were predicted in the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>We then compared the genomes of 26 plant species to obtain gene families that are significantly expanded in Lauraceae or that are unique to Lauraceae (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">4b, c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">11</a>). Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) and Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analyses found that the significantly expanded gene families are especially enriched in the KEGG pathways of monoterpenoid biosynthesis, biosynthesis of secondary metabolites, and metabolic (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">12</a>) and in the GO terms of TPS activity, transferase activity, and catalytic activity (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">13</a>). Many monoterpene synthase (TPS-b) genes are included in the above enriched KEGG pathways and GO terms, in line with the roles of TPS-b in the biosynthesis of specific scents (mainly monoterpene) in Lauraceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1183">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Chang, Y. T. &amp; Chu, F. H. Molecular cloning and characterization of monoterpene synthases from Litsea cubeba (Lour.) Persoon. Tree Genet. Genomes 7, 835–844 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1186">24</a></sup>. The enrichment analyses showed that the 711 unique Lauraceae gene families are specifically enriched in the KEGG pathways of plant hormone signal transduction and circadian rhythm—plant (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">14</a>) and in the GO terms of regulation of cellular metabolic and organic cyclic compound metabolic processes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">15</a>). Hormone-related transcriptional factors are over-presented in the unique gene families to Lauraceae, for example, ABSCISIC ACID-INSENSITIVE 5 (ABI5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Finkelstein, R. R. &amp; Lynch, T. J. The Arabidopsis abscisic acid response gene ABI5 encodes a basic leucine zipper transcription factor. Plant Cell 12, 599–609 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1197">25</a></sup> and ethylene-responsive transcription factor ERF098. Furthermore, the species-specific gene families of <i>L. cubeba</i> are significantly enriched in the KEGG pathways of biosynthesis of terpenoids and steroids and nitrogen metabolism, and the gene families under significant expansion in <i>L. cubeba</i> include many members from the ABC transporter C family, which is generally involved in the membrane transport of the secondary metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Yazaki, K. ABC transporters involved in the transport of plant secondary metabolites. FEBS Lett. 580, 1183–1191 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1207">26</a></sup> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">21</a>). It is interesting to notice that the TPS and ABC transporter members form gene clusters on chromosomes 8 and 12 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">The phylogenetic position of Magnoliids among angiosperm</h3><p>Laurales are an order of Magnoliids, whose evolutionary position, mainly with respect to eudicots and monocots, is still the object of contention<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Massoni, J., Couvreur, T. L. P. &amp; Sauquet, H. Five major shifts of diversification through the long evolutionary history of magnoliidae (angiosperms). BMC Evol. Biol. 15, 49 (2015)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1229">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species-pair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18–25 (2018)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1229_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1229_2">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Nuclear genomes of two Magnoliids. Nat. Plants 5, 6–7 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1232">13</a></sup>. On the basis of the 160 single-copy gene families derived from 19 eudicots, 8 monocots, 4 Magnoliids, and 3 outgroup species (<i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Ginkgo biloba</i>, and <i>Anthoceros punctatus</i>), we constructed phylogenetic trees from the concatenated sequence alignments of both nucleotide and amino acids sequences (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1a, c</a>, left side). In these analyses, Magnoliids were found as a sister group to eudicots after their common ancestor diverged from monocots, which agrees with a previous study using the <i>C. kanehirae</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1252">12</a></sup>. To reduce the possibility of long branch attraction in our phylogenetic analysis, we conducted another phylogenomic analysis without Gramineae species and obtained the same topology (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Concatenated- and ASTRAL-based phylogenetic trees."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Concatenated- and ASTRAL-based phylogenetic trees.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="552" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Phylogenetic trees based on the concatenated (left) and multi-species coalescent (MSC) methods (right) using nucleotide sequences. Magnoliids are indicated with a gray background. Red stars with labels I, II, III, and IV refer to the discussions on phylogenetic discordances (see text). <b>b</b> Estimated proportions of the 160 single-copy gene trees with different topologies based on nucleotide alignments. The <i>x</i>-axis labels q1, q2, and q3 refer to the quartet support for the main topology (red), the first alternative (blue), and the second alternative (yellow), respectively. The dashed line refers to a proportion of 0.33. <b>c</b> Phylogenetic trees based on the concatenated (left) and MSC methods (right) using amino acid sequences. Interpretation is as in <b>a</b>. <b>d</b> Estimated proportions of the 160 single-copy gene trees based on amino acid sequences. Interpretation is same as <b>b</b>. Source data underlying (<b>a</b>) and (<b>c</b>) are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Because incomplete lineage sorting (ILS) may play a role in confounding resolution of early-diverging branches within angiosperms, such as the divergence of Magnoliids, eudicots, and monocots, we further conducted the multi-species coalescent (MSC)-based phylogenomic analyses using ASTRAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mirarab, S. et al. ASTRAL: genome-scale coalescent-based species tree estimation. Bioinformatics 30, i541–i548 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1301">27</a></sup> by considering each gene tree from the 160 single-copy gene families separately (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1a, c</a>, right side). The MSC-based phylogeny using nucleotides (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1a</a>, right side) again supports a sister group relationship between Magnoliids and eudicots, to the exclusion of monocots. However, the MSC-based tree using amino acids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1c</a>, right side) suggests Magnoliids to form a sister group with monocots, after their divergence from eudicots. To evaluate the discordance of gene trees in our single-copy gene data set, we used the <i>Q</i> value in ASTRAL to display the percentages of gene trees in support of the main topology (q1), and the first (q2) and second (q3) alternative topologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mirarab, S. et al. ASTRAL: genome-scale coalescent-based species tree estimation. Bioinformatics 30, i541–i548 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1318">27</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1b, d</a>). For example, the majority of gene trees inferred by both nucleotide and amino acid sequences support Amborellales being a sister group to the other angiosperms as the main topology (q1), while there are few gene trees that support either monocots (q2) or eudicots (q3) being the sister group to other angiosperms, respectively (I in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1b, d</a>). In contrast, the branching order for Magnoliids, monocots, and eudicots displays a high level of discordance among the single-copy gene trees, with two nearly equally supported (and one slightly less supported) topologies in both nucleotide and amino acid sequences-based analyses (II in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1b-II, d-II</a>).</p><p>Other discordances among the single-copy gene trees analyzed with ASTRAL concerned the phylogenetic position of <i>Vitis vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Van de Peer, Y., Mizrachi, E. &amp; Marchal, K. The evolutionary significance of polyploidy. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 411–424 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1336">28</a></sup> and the phylogenetic position of <i>P. americana</i> (left side in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1b, d</a>). All phylogenomic analyses focusing on Lauraceae species support a sister relationship of <i>Litsea</i> and <i>Cinnamomum</i> (right side in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1b, d</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Whole-genome duplications in Laurales</h3><p>Genome collinearity and paralog age distributions all show indications of two ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) events for <i>L. cubeba</i>. Intragenomic analysis of gene order reveals collinear regions with up to five (but mostly two to four) paralogous segments (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">22</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">8</a>), while age distributions of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>S</sub>) for all paralogous genes (paranome), as well as duplicates retained in collinear regions (anchor pairs) both show two signature peaks for WGD events with a recent peak at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> ≈ 0.5 and a more ancient peak at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> ≈ 0.8 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2a, b</a>). Similarly, previously sequenced genomes (of <i>C. kanehirae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1391">12</a></sup> and <i>P. americana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Rendón-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1398">14</a></sup>) and transcriptomes (from this study and 1KP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="One Thousand Plant Transcriptomes Initiative. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature 574, 679–685 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1402">29</a></sup>) of Lauraceae also show two signature peaks in their paranome <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions, except for <i>Cassytha filiformis</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a>), for which only one signature peak could be identified. WGD analyses using the <i>C. kanehirae</i> genome suggested that the recent WGD in <i>C. kanehirae</i> is shared by all the Lauraceae species except <i>C. filiformis</i>, while the ancient WGD seems shared by Laurales and Magnoliales, i.e., the two clades that form a sister group in the Magnoliid clade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1428">12</a></sup>. Also, the analysis of the genome of <i>L. chinense</i>, another species in the order of Magnoliales, supported a WGD prior to the divergence between Laurales and Magnoliales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Singh, R. &amp; Jawaid, T. Cinnamomum camphora (Kapur): review. Pharmacogn. J. 4, 1–5 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1435">8</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Whole-genome duplications in Laurales."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Whole-genome duplications in Laurales.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="512" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> age distribution for the whole paranome of <i>L. cubeba</i>. Two <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peaks are shown by dotted lines at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> ≈ 0.5 and 0.8 falling in two <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> ranges highlighted by two gray rectangles in the background from 0.3 to 0.645 and from 0.645 to 1.1, respectively. <b>b</b> <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> age distributions for anchor pairs of <i>L. cubeba</i> (dark gray histogram and line; peaks represent WGD events) and for one-to-one orthologs between <i>L. cubeba</i> and selected Lauralean species and <i>V. vinifera</i> (colored filled curves of kernel-density estimates; a peak represents a species divergence event). The arrows in different colors indicate under- (to the left) and overestimations (to the right) of the divergence events and point to the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> values after corrections of different substitution rates in the three comparisons based on that in <i>L. cubeba</i> (see Methods). <b>c</b> The phylogeny of Laurales and Magnoliales with branch lengths in <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> units (left) and in absolute divergence time (right). The tree topology and absolute divergence time were retrieved. The <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> ages and their 95% confidence intervals (CIs) for WGDs identified from the whole paranome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a>) are shown in dots and rectangles, respectively, on the left phylogeny. Without considering species with only one <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peak in their <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions of paralogs, the blue and green rectangles highlight the ranges of 95% CIs of WGDs for the older peaks and the younger peaks, respectively. The red, light blue, and yellow rectangles show the ranges of 95% CIs for three independent WGD events in different lineages of Laurales. Correspondingly, the red, light blue, and yellow bars illustrate the independent WGD events on the right phylogenetic tree with absolute divergence time. The three WGD events occurred at about the same time of the radiation of Lauralean species within ~3 million years. In addition, the green bar denotes a lineage-specific WGD event in <i>C. filiformis</i> and the blue bar denotes the WGD event before the divergence of Laurales and Magnoliales.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Our analyses of the transcriptomes of non-Lauraceae Lauralean species, however, found that although some of these species have only one WGD peak in their paranome <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions, others, such as <i>Peumus boldus</i>, <i>Laurelia sempervirens</i>, <i>Gomortega keule</i>, and <i>Chimonanthus praecox</i>, have two such peaks (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a>). Specifically, compared with those in <i>L. cubeba</i>, the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> values of the two WGD peaks are larger in <i>C. praecox</i> and smaller in the other three species, suggesting that the signature peaks in these species may result from either different WGD events or from various substitution rates for duplicates retained following the same WGD events. Indeed, the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions of one-to-one orthologs identified between <i>V. vinifera</i> and species from Laurales and Magnoliales show different <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peaks for the divergence event between <i>V. vinifera</i> and Magnoliids, indicating different substitution rates within Laurales (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">10</a>). In short, our results provide evidence of two WGD events not only in the Lauraceae species but also in some non-Lauraceae species in the Laurales order.</p><p>To better position the two WGD events identified in the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome in the lineage of Laurales, we compared the anchor-pair <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distribution of <i>L. cubeba</i> with the orthologous <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2b</a>): (1) between <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>Dehaasia hainanensis</i>, <i>G. keule</i>, and <i>C. praecox</i> to represent the divergence of different lineages in Laurales (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2c</a>); (2) between <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>L. chinense</i> to represent the divergence between Laurales and Magnoliales; and (3) between <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> to represent the divergence between Magnoliids and eudicots. Both the analysis of <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions and relative rate tests to correct for different substitution rates in different species of Laurales (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2</a>) suggest that the ancient <i>L. cubeba</i> WGD has occurred shortly before the divergence of Laurales and Magnoliales, while the recent WGD has occurred before the divergence of Lauraceae but closely following the divergence of the lineage including <i>C. praecox</i> and the lineage including <i>G. keule</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2b, c</a>). Some species, such as <i>L. sempervirens</i>, <i>G. keule</i>, and <i>C. praecox</i>, have not experienced the recent WGD identified in <i>L. cubeba</i>, but also show two signature peaks for WGDs in their paranome <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a>). Considering <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peak values (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2</a>), we infer independent WGDs in three different lineages of Laurales: one in the lineage leading to <i>C. praecox</i> and <i>Idiospermum australiense</i>; one in the lineage leading to <i>L. sempervirens</i> and <i>G. keule</i>; and another in the lineage, including Lauraceae, <i>P. boldus</i>, and possibly <i>G. americanus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2c</a>). Interestingly, <i>C. filiformis</i>, an obligate parasitic plant in the Lauraceae, and the one with the highest substitution rate in our analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">10</a>), show a <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peak that represents a lineage-specific WGD event after its divergence from other Lauraceae species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2c</a>). However, we propose that <i>C. filiformis</i> shares the same WGD history as <i>L. cubeba</i> and the other Lauraceae, but draws a different picture because of its accelerated substitution rate responsible for diminishing the signature peaks for the two WGDs in its paranome <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution.</p><p>Lauralean species must have experienced rapid radiation over ~3 million years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Suleski, M. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree: a resource for timelines, timetrees, and divergence times. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1812–1819 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1761">30</a></sup>. Interestingly, the younger WGD peaks in Laurales seem to coincide with such a period (the right-hand tree in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2c</a>), which could imply that these WGD events might even have facilitated the rapid radiation of the early Lauralean species. On the other hand, it cannot be ruled that there has been only one WGD event that has occurred shortly before the rapid radiation of Laurales. Under such scenario, our observation of three independent WGDs could be explained by one single WGD that has occurred just before the divergence of Laurales followed by independent diploidizations in the three Lauralean lineages during species radiation, with similarity to the process described in the “lineage-specific ohnologue resolution” model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Robertson, F. M. et al. Lineage-specific rediploidization is a mechanism to explain time-lags between genome duplication and evolutionary diversification. Genome Biol. 18, 111 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1768">31</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">The evolution of floral structures in Lauraceae</h3><p>To investigate the evolution of floral structures in Lauraceae, we first inferred the phylogenetic relationships within Lauraceae using both the concatenated and MSC approaches based on single-copy genes identified from the transcriptomes of 22 species representing 16 genera (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">11</a>, and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">23</a>). We also obtained a plastid phylogeny based on the reconstructed plastid genomes from 27 species representing 19 genera in Lauraceae (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">5</a> Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">24</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">25</a>). Phylogenetic trees reconstructed from concatenated sequence alignments had similar topologies than the MSC trees, except for the position of <i>Lindera</i> and <i>Laurus</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">12</a>). Comparing the nuclear and plastid trees, however, we identified notable differences for some genera in Lauraceae, such as <i>Lindera</i>, <i>Laurus</i>, <i>Nothaphoebe</i>, <i>Phoebe</i>, <i>Dehaasia</i>, <i>Persea</i>, and <i>Alseodaphne</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">6</a>). ASTRAL analysis also shows phylogenetic discordance among gene trees for the discordant nodes between the nuclear tree and plastid tree (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">12</a>), indicating a complicated evolutionary history of Lauraceae. Specifically, <i>Cryptocarya</i> is the sister group to other Lauraceae species in the plastid tree, while in both the concatenated and MSC trees based on nuclear genes, <i>Cassytha</i> is a sister to other Lauraceae species. Similar differences have been reported in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rohwer, J. G. Toward a phylogenetic classification of the Lauraceae: evidence from matK sequences. Syst. Bot. 25, 60–71 (2000)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1849">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chanderbali, A. S., van der Werff, H. &amp; Renner, S. S. Phylogeny and historical biogeography of Lauraceae: evidence from the chloroplast and nuclear Genomes. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 88, 104–134 (2001)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1849_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Song, Y. et al. Evolutionary comparisons of the chloroplast genome in Lauraceae and insights into loss events in the Magnoliids. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 2354–2364 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1852">34</a></sup>, at least in our ASTRAL analysis; strong support is given to the sister relationship between <i>Cassytha</i> and other Lauraceae species with few discordant gene trees with respect to <i>Cassytha</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">6</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The evolution of floral structures in Lauraceae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: The evolution of floral structures in Lauraceae.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="546" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Phylogeny of Lauraceae based on a concatenated sequence alignment of 275 single-copy gene families for 22 species in the Lauraceae. <b>b</b> The variable panicles in Lauraceae (from bottom to up): spikes in <i>Cassytha</i>, spikelike panicles in the <i>Cryptocarya</i> group, cymoses panicles in the <i>Alseodaphne</i>-<i>Phoebe</i> clade and <i>Cinnamomum</i>, pseudo-umbel in <i>Sassafras</i>, and umbels in the <i>Laurus</i>-<i>Litsea</i> clade. <b>c</b> Perianth tube turbinate or suburceolate present in <i>Cryptocarya</i> group and <i>Cassytha</i>. <i>Caryodaphnopsis</i> and <i>Alseodaphne</i>-<i>Phoebe</i> clade appear broadly conical and short perianth tube. Perianth tubes are campanulate, short to nearly absent in the <i>Cinnamomum</i>-<i>Litsea</i> clade. <b>d</b> The <i>Cinnamomum</i>-<i>Litsea</i> clade has unisexual flowers and the other species in Lauraceae have bio-sexual flowers. <b>e</b> The phylogenetic tree of FUWA homologs in different Lauraceae species. <b>f</b> <i>PTL</i> expression in the flower buds of Lauraceae species. The <i>PTL</i> expression level was noted as being consistent with the variation of perianth morphology in Lauraceae. <i>PTL</i> exhibited a higher level of expression in the flower buds of the basic group lineage (<i>Cryptocarya</i> group), which presented an abscission of the perianth tube from the perianth tube encapsulated in fruits. <i>PTL</i> had a lower level of expression in the <i>Litsea</i>-<i>Cinnamomum</i> clade, where the fruit receptacle developed from the perianth tube. <b>g</b> TGACG motif-binding protein family member <i>TGA10</i> has higher transcriptional expression level in male flowers than that in female flowers from eight unisexual species representing four genera, and <i>TGA10</i> also has higher expression lever in male flowers comparing with that in bisexual flowers from nine bisexual species representing six genera of Lauraceae. Source data underlying <b>a</b>, <b>e</b>–<b>g</b> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Generally, the inflorescences in Lauraceae are panicles, spikes, racemes, pseudo-umbels, and umbels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li, S. G. et al. Lauraceae. FOC 7, 102–153 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65370e1993">2</a></sup>. These characteristics of inflorescences are of importance to the classification of the Lauraceae family (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3b</a>). To investigate the genes that are potentially involved in the evolution of inflorescences, we considered the transcriptomes of flower buds for 21 different species (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">26</a>) and phylogeny of Lauraceae inferred by MrBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Huelsenbeck, J. P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754–755 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2006">35</a></sup> were conducted (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3a</a>). In particular, we focused on the <i>FUWA</i> genes in 13 genera, because <i>FUWA</i> has been reported to play an essential role in determining panicle architecture in rice, sorghum, and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Chen, J. et al. An evolutionarily conserved gene, FUWA, plays a role in determining panicle architecture, grain shape and grain weight in rice. Plant J. 83, 427–438 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2020">36</a></sup>. A phylogenetic tree based on the orthologs of FUWA in different Lauraceae species (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">8</a>) seems consistent with the evolution of inflorescences (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3a, b,</a> and e). The <i>Cassytha</i> and <i>Cryptocarya</i> group, which diverged earlier than other Lauraceae lineages, have spike and spikelike panicles, respectively, which are usually referred to as irregular panicles. Subsequently, regular panicles and umbels are present in other Lauraceae species. For example, cymose panicles appear in the <i>Alseodaphne</i>-<i>Phoebe</i> clade, pseudo-umbels appear in <i>Sassafras</i>, and umbels appear in the <i>Litsea</i>-<i>Laurus</i> clade. Consistently, the analyses of domain architectures of the <i>FUWA</i> gene indicate that the gene contains two conserved NHL domains in the early-diverging lineages of the <i>Cryptocarya</i> Group and <i>Cassytha</i> and three NHL domains in other Lauraceae species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3e</a>). A similar pattern has also been observed in Lauraceae for involucres. In Lauraceae, genera with panicles and racemes have no involucre, while Sassafras with pseudo-umbels has bracts linear to filamentous, and the <i>Litsea</i>-<i>Laurus</i> clade with umbels has an obvious involucre. The inflorescences morphological differentiation could be related to the geographic distribution of Lauraceae. The <i>Cryptocarya</i> Group and <i>Cassytha</i> are found in the Southern Hemisphere, while the other clades are mainly distributed in the amphi-Pacific or Asian areas.</p><p>The evolution of perianth tubes in Lauraceae also seems to mirror the phylogeny of Lauraceae (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3a, c</a>). It has been known that the loss of the trihelix transcription factor PETAL LOSS (PTL) could induce the disruption of perianth development in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Brewer, P. B. et al. PETAL LOSS, a trihelix transcription factor gene, regulates perianth architecture in the Arabidopsis flower. Development 131, 4035–4045 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2086">37</a></sup>. Therefore, the differences in <i>PTL</i> expression in Lauraceae could be consistent with the variations of perianth tubes. Indeed, comparing with the <i>Litsea</i>-<i>Cinnamomum</i> clade, where the perianth tubes are indistinct, short, and campanulate, <i>PTL</i> genes from other Lauracea clades exhibit higher level of expression in the flower buds and these species have perianth tubes turbinate or suburceolate (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3c, f</a>).</p><p>The most recent common ancestor of Laurales was a tree with actinomorphic and bisexual flowers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Massoni, J. Phylogeny, molecular dating and floral evolution of magnoliidae (Angiospermae). Vegetal Biology. Université Paris Sud-Paris XI (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2112">38</a></sup>. Extant Lauraceae species include both bisexual (dioecious) and unisexual (monoecious) species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">1</a>). To identify the genes involved in the sexual determination in Lauraceae, we produced and integrated Illumina transcriptome data for flower buds from 17 species in 10 genera of Lauraceae, including 8 unisexual species in 4 genera and 9 bisexual species in 6 genera (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">26</a>). The comparative analyses of the transcriptome data illustrate that the differentially expressed genes (DEGs) between unisexual and bisexual species are enriched in the KEGG pathway of plant hormone signal transduction. Among these genes, <i>TGA10</i> shows obviously higher expression in male flowers from monoecious species than both female flowers from monoecious species and bisexual flowers from dioecious species in Lauraceae (see “Methods,” Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">10</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3d, g</a>). Our results hence suggest that <i>TGA10</i> is involved in male flower development, which is consistent with studies that have found that <i>TGA10</i> is required for anther development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Murmu, J. et al. Arabidopsis basic leucine-zipper transcription factors TGA9 and TGA10 interact with floral glutaredoxins ROXY1 and ROXY2 and are redundantly required for anther development. Plant Physiol. 154, 1492–1504 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2144">39</a></sup>. Moreover, a hypothesized protein (Lcu01G_02292) may also have contributed to sexual determination in Lauraceae, considering their differential expression patterns in bisexual and unisexual flowers in Lauraceae (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">14</a>). We also compared the MADS-box genes in the sequenced Lauraceae species and a few other angiosperms (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">11</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">15</a>, and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">27</a>). Notably, we found that <i>SOC1</i>-like genes are expanded in both <i>L. cubeba</i> (seven members of <i>SOC1</i>) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">28</a>) and <i>C. kanehirae</i> (eight members of <i>SOC1</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2180">12</a></sup>. Consistent with the expanded <i>SOC1</i> clade, the <i>SVP</i> clade is also expanded and it counts five members in <i>L. cubeba</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">15</a>). It has been reported that the interaction of <i>SOC1</i> and <i>AGL24</i> from the SVP clade integrates flowering signals in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Liu, C. et al. Direct interaction of AGL24 and SOC1 integrates flowering signals in Arabidopsis. Development 135, 1481–1491 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2206">40</a></sup>. Both the expanded <i>SOC1</i> and <i>SVP</i> clades could be involved in complex flowering regulation networks and could relate to differential regulation of dioecious plant flowering.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Mono-TPS involved in volatiles production in Lauraceae</h3><p>The essential oils produced by Lauraceae are widely used commercially, and contain a variety of components (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">29</a>), such as geranial, neral, limonene, and linalool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kilic, A., Hafizoglu, H., Kollmannsberger, H. &amp; Nitz, S. Volatile constituents and key odorants in leaves, buds, flowers, and fruits of Laurus nobilis L. J. Agr. Food Chem. 52, 1601–1606 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2228">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chaw, S. M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2231">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Huang, X. W., Feng, Y. C. &amp; Huang, Y. Potential cosmetic application of essential oil extracted from Litsea cubeba fruits from China. J. Essent. Oil Res. 25, 112–119 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2234">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Su, Y. C. &amp; Ho, C. L. Essential oil compositions and antimicrobial activities of various parts of Litsea cubeba from Taiwan. Nat. Prod. Commun. 11, 515–518 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2237">20</a></sup>. TPSs are the rate-limiting enzymes in the production of such terpenoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E.
