The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 66, 212–229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2241">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tholl, D. Biosynthesis and biological functions of terpenoids in plants. Adv. Biochem. Eng. Biotechnol. 148, 63–106 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2244">41</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig4">4</a>). The present gene family analysis suggests that the TPS-b gene clade is significantly expanded in Lauraceae (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">13</a>). We hence identified all the TPS genes in Lauraceae by combining the data for the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome and the transcriptome data for 23 species, from 16 genera, in the Lauraceae family (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">30</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">31</a>). Lauraceae species with a high percentage content of essential oil had larger numbers of TPS-b members (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">30</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">31</a>), for example, <i>L. cubeba</i> possessed 24 TPS-b genes in (3–7%, the percentage of essential oil in fresh fruit), <i>Cinnamomum verum</i> possessed 12 TPS-b genes (1.32–2.13%, the percentage of essential oil in fresh leaves), <i>Machilus salicina</i> possessed 13 TPS-b genes (1.05%, the percentage of essential oil in fresh leaves), and <i>P. americana</i> possessed 17 TPS-b genes (1%, the percentage of essential oil in fresh ripe fruit)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Rendón-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2286">14</a></sup> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">30</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">31</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Scent biosynthesis in Lauraceae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Scent biosynthesis in Lauraceae.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="698" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Tissue-specific relative expression profiles (red–blue scale) of genes implicated in terpenoid biosynthesis (heat map). Intermediates are shown in black, and the enzymes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">34</a>) involved at each step are shown in gray. The genes involved in the MEP pathway exhibit a high level of fruit-development-specific expression, which may contribute to the biosynthesis of large amounts of monoterpenes. SesquiTPSs, or the responsible sesquiterpene biosynthesis of flowers, involve the gene expansion of 17 members (full amino acid length &gt;200 aa). MonoTPSs involved in the production of monoterpenes in fruits also show signs of family expansion for 27 members (full amino acid length &gt;200 aa) of <i>L. cubeba</i>. <i>LcuTPSs</i> form a gene cluster in chromosome 8 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">5</a>). MVA pathway mevalonate pathway, MEP pathway mevalonate-independent (deoxyxylulose phosphate) pathway, R root, S stem, L leaves, FL flower, F1 fruit 40 days after full bloom, F2 fruit 70 days after full bloom, F3 fruit 100 days after full bloom, F4 fruit 140 days after full bloom. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We analyzed the first key enzyme in the scent biosynthetic pathway, namely 1-deoxyxylulose 5-phosphate synthase (DXS), which has three clades (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">16</a>). The members in the clade B are expanded across <i>Litsea</i>, <i>Beilschmiedia</i>, and <i>Sassafras</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">16</a>); as a result, Lauraceae produce high levels of terpenoids. <i>LcuDXS3-5</i> belongs to clade B and exhibits very high expression in fruits according to the transcriptome data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Han, X. J. et al. Transcriptome sequencing and expression analysis of terpenoid biosynthesis genes in Litsea cubeba. PLoS ONE 8, e76890 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2343">42</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig4">4</a>). Furthermore, transient overexpression of <i>LcuDXS3</i> in <i>L. cubeba</i> could induce the increase of several components of monoterpene and sesquiterpene (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">17</a>).</p><p>To further investigate the members of TPS-b and TPS-g involved in terpenoid biosynthesis, additional expression and functional verification studies were conducted in <i>L. cubeba</i>. Among the 52 full-length TPS genes in <i>L. cubeba</i>, 27 were predicted as monoterpene synthase genes, 17 as sesquiterpene synthase genes, and the remaining 8 as diterpene synthase genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">32</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">34</a>). Tandem duplication (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig5">5a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">5</a>) events have contributed to the expansion of the TPS-b subfamilies. Both Illumina transcriptome sequencing data (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig4">4</a>) and qRT-PCR verification showed (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">17</a>) that <i>LcuTPS22</i> specifically accumulated in leaves. Transient overexpression and enzyme activity assay both demonstrated that LcuTPS22 catalyzed the accumulation of α-pinene, β-pinene, eucalyptol, camphene, eucalyptol, and camphor, which are the main volatile components of the leaves of Lauraceae species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig5">5</a>). In addition, <i>LcuTPS18</i>, <i>19</i>, <i>20</i>, <i>25</i>, <i>26</i>, and <i>42</i> were all highly expressed in the <i>L. cubeba</i> fruits (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">17</a>). Transient overexpression and enzyme activity assay also indicated that LcuTPS42 catalyzed the biosynthesis of linalool, phellandrene, and geraniol, which are the main components of the specific scents in Lauraceae (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig5">5</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Phylogeny and functional verification of LcuTPSs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Phylogeny and functional verification of LcuTPSs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-15493-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_15493_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="822" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> Phylogeny of TPSs. Putative full-length TPS proteins (&gt;200 amino acids in length) identified in <i>L. cubeba</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">32</a>) and 10 other sequenced plant genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">33</a>) were subjected to phylogenetic analysis. TPS subfamilies are shown along the circumference of the circle. <b>b</b> Transient overexpression of <i>LcuTPSs</i> in <i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i> leaves. After infiltration, the plants were grown for 2 days and the presence or concentration of the monoterpenes was detected using GC-MS analysis. Data represent the mean ± SDs of three biological replicates. <b>c</b> Identification of enzymatic products after incubating recombinant LcuTPSs proteins with geranyl diphosphate. The recombined enzyme expressed in <i>Escherichia coli</i> was purified by Ni<sup>2+</sup> affinity. The volatile terpenes were further analyzed by GC-MS analysis comparison with authentic standards (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">18</a>). Source data underling <b>a</b>, <b>b</b> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Besides the TPS-b gene families, other genes may also function as mediators in scent biosynthesis in <i>L. cubeba</i>. For example, plant hormone signal transduction enrichment of gene families is unique to Lauraceae; therefore, we investigated the endogenous hormone content and found that abscisic acid (ABA) had a unique peak close to that of the biosynthesized monoterpene in <i>L. cubeba</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">19a</a>). Correspondingly, the treatment of <i>L. cubeba</i> leaves with ABA induced an increase in both the level of monoterpene (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">19b</a>) and the expression of <i>LcuTPS22</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">19c</a>). In summary, our results provide insights into the candidate genes involved in scent production in the Lauraceae family. Further studies are, however, required to elucidate the mechanisms underlying the regulation of these genes in the scent biosynthetic pathways in Lauraceae.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>It seemed that the evolutionary relationships between Magnoliids, monocots, and eudicots as still unresolved, since our ASTRAL analyses suggest the possible ILS during the rapid divergence of early mesangiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="One Thousand Plant Transcriptomes Initiative. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature 574, 679–685 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2517">29</a></sup>. ILS is a result of polymorphic alleles in the ancestral populations. Despite nucleotide polymorphisms, prevalent copy number variations could also exist in the ancestral populations and exacerbate the effects of ILS, as suggested by gene count data used to infer the phylogenetic position of Magnoliids in a previous study on the <i>P. americana</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Rendón-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2524">14</a></sup>. Besides sequence data, approaches based on synteny data could perhaps provide additional evidence with respect to the evolutionary relationship of Magnoliids. For example, utilizing sequence dissimilarities of orthologous genes located on orthologous synteny blocks, synteny-based phylogenetic analyses for <i>P. americana</i> supported Magnoliids as the sister group to monocots and eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Rendón-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2531">14</a></sup>. Recent analyses by some of us<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Zhao, T. et al. Novel phylogeny of angiosperms inferred from whole-genome microsynteny analysis. bioRxiv Preprint at &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.15.908376&#xA;&#xA; (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2536">43</a></sup>, considering phylogenies based on the relative gene order using synteny network data, rather than gene sequence similarities, suggested a sister group relationship between Magnoliids and monocots, thereby supporting the MSC (ASTRAL) tree based on amino acid sequence alignments (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1c</a>).</p><p>The results of the present analyses of the key enzymes involved in monoterpene biosynthesis pathway would hence suggest that the duplications of <i>DXS</i> and <i>TPS</i> gene families may have led to the separation of biological features in terpenoid production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 66, 212–229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2552">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Saladie, M. et al. The 2-C-methylerythritol 4-phosphate pathway in melon is regulated by specialized isoforms for the first and last steps. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 5077–5092 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2555">44</a></sup>, and gene overexpression may enhance the production of main components in terpene (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig5">5</a>). Therefore, we propose that gene family sizes and regulation of gene expression both contribute to the accumulation of terpenoid in <i>L. cubeba</i>. As reported in a recent publication, following expansion of the TPS gene family, gene cluster formation, gene functional differentiation, and gene regulation divergence all could contribute to the variations in terpene production and concentration among individual species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Chen, H. et al. Combinatorial evolution of a terpene synthase gene cluster explains terpene variations in. Oryza. N. Phytol. 182, 480–492 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2566">45</a></sup>. In addition, new functions gained by recently duplicated TPS genes may be correlated with recently evolved terpene compounds that are capable of defending plants against biotic and abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Pichersky, E. &amp; Raguso, R. A. Why do plants produce so many terpenoid compounds. N. Phytol. 220, 692–702 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2570">46</a></sup>.</p><p>In summary, the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome provides a valuable resource for elucidating the Lauraceae evolution toolkit. Most of the present phylogenetic analyses suggest a sister group relationship of Magnoliids and eudicots, after the divergence of their common ancestor from monocots. However, the exact evolutionary relationships between Magnoliids, monocots, and dicots remain to be solved because topological conflict suggests substantial ILS in the (short) branches separating these three groups. Phylogenetic inference also suggests that the obligatory parasitic species <i>Cassytha</i> is sister to the other Lauraceae. In the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome, remnants of two ancient WGD events could be detected. In addition, comparative and functional analyses showed that TPS-b genes were significantly expanded and responsible for terpenoid biosynthesis in Lauraceae. In conclusion, our data offer insight into the genetic diversity and evolution of Laurales—and the scents they produce—and provide a stronger understanding of the evolution and diversification of Lauraceae.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Sample preparation and sequencing</h3><p>For genome sequencing, we collected tissues from <i>L. cubeba</i> in Zhejiang Province (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">1</a>), China, with a karyotype of 2<i>n</i> = 24 and with uniform and small chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2a</a>). Genomic DNA was isolated from the buds of <i>L. cubeba</i>, and at least 10 μg of sheared DNA was taken. SMRTbell template preparation involved DNA concentration, damage repair, end repair, ligation of hairpin adapters, and template purification, undertaken using AMPure PB Magnetic Beads (Pacific Biosciences). Finally, we carried out 20 kb single-molecule real-time DNA sequencing using PacBio to sequence a DNA library on the PacBio Sequel platform, yielding ~213 Gb PacBio data (read quality ≥0.80 and mean read length ≥7 kb) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly and assessment</h3><p>The assembly of <i>L. cubeba</i> genome was conducted using PacBio and 10× Genomics Linked-Reads. De novo assembly of the PacBio reads was performed using FALCON (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/</a>) with single-molecule, real-time sequencing. Briefly, the longest 60× coverage of subreads were selected as seeds to do error correction with parameters “--output_multi --min_idt 0.70 --min_cov 4 -- max_n_read 300.” The corrected reads were then aligned to each other to construct string graphs with parameters “--length_cutoff_pr 11000.” The graph was further flited with parameters “--max_diff 70 --max_cov 70 --min_cov 3” and contigs were finally generated according to these graphs. The primary contigs were then polished using Quiver by aligning SMRT reads. The total length of this assembly was 1700.7 Mb, with a contig N50 of 460.7 kb. The total length of this assembly was significantly longer than the estimated genome size (1370.1 Mb), which indicated that several redundant sequences were presented in the assembly. This was confirmed by the large proportion of BUSCO duplicated genes, with 21.3% of the BUSCO genes duplicated in the assembly. We, therefore, undertook a redundancy filtering step for the assembly. The removal of genome hybrids was achieved by a purge of haplotigs, which provided a pipeline to the reassignment of allelic contigs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Roach, M. J., Schmidt, S. A. &amp; Borneman, A. R. Purge Haplotigs: allelic contig reassignment for third-gen diploid genome assemblies. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 460 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2632">47</a></sup>. Briefly, the pipeline first identified putative heterozygous contigs through read-depth analysis. Contigs with a high proportion of bases within the 0.5× read-depth peak were assigned as putative heterozygous contigs. These putative heterozygous contigs were then subject to a sequence alignment to identify its allelic companion contig. Then the identified haplotigs were removed from the assembly iteratively. We attempted several cutoffs for the pipeline, then selected a genome version that was balanced between redundancy and integrity. The total length of the filtered genome assembly was 1315.8 Mb, with a contig N50 of 603.7 kb. The filtered assembly was then connected by 10× genomics data. We used the BWA-ME algorithm (Toward better understanding of artifacts in variant calling from high-coverage samples) to align the 143.28 Gb 10× genomics data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">1</a>) by the default setting and the scaffolding was performed by FragScaff (A hybrid approach for de novo human genome sequence assembly and phasing) using the alignment file as the input. We selected on the parameters of <i>j</i> = 1.25 and <i>u</i> = 2 to achieve a relatively conservative assembly and minimize the introduction of scaffolding errors. After that, we used PBjelly (Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with PacificBiosciences RS long-read sequencing technology) software to fill gaps with PacBio data. The options were -minMatch 8 -sdpTupleSize 8 -minPctIdentity 75 -bestn 1 -nCandidates 10 -maxScore −500 -nproc 13 -noSplitSubreads. In order to get enough corrected genome sequences, we used Pilon (v1.18) with default settings to do the second round of error correction. For the input BAM file, we used BWA (Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM) to align total 359.77 Gb Illumina short reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">1</a>) to the assembly and used SAMTOOLS (The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools) to sort and index the BAM file. Finally, we obtained a genome with a contig N50 size of 607.34 kb and a scaffold N50 size of 1.76 Mb. The total length of this genome version was 1325.59 Mb, and it contained 0.90% Ns (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>To confirm the quality of the genome assembly, we performed a BUSCO (<a href="http://busco.ezlab.org/">http://busco.ezlab.org/</a>) assessment using 1440 single-copy orthologous genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">5</a>), and we found a genome completeness value of 88.4%. To confirm the high coverage of the assembly, we mapped available mRNA sequences to the assembled genome using BLAST software (<a href="http://genome.ucsc.edu/goldenpath/help/blatSpec.html">http://genome.ucsc.edu/goldenpath/help/blatSpec.html</a>). In total, 32,499 (97.91%) were supported by transcriptome data, with a sequence coverage &gt;50% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Hi-C library construction and assembly of the chromosome</h3><p>For Hi-C libraries construction, 3 g seedlings were crosslinked with 40 ml 2% formaldehyde solution at room temperature for 30 min in a vacuum. Glycine (2.5 M) was added to quench the crosslinking reaction. After fixation, 0.5 g of fixed tissue was ground with liquid nitrogen for the first round of library preparation. The extracted nuclei were resuspended with 50 μl 0.5% SDS followed by incubation at 62 °C for 10 min, and then SDS molecules were quenched by adding 25 μl 10% Triton X-100 and incubated at 37 °C for 20 min. For following restriction digestion in intact nuclei, DNA was digested with the four-cutter restriction enzyme DpnII and incubated at 37 °C overnight. The DpnII enzyme was inactivated at 62 °C for 20 min. The cohesive ends were filled and marked with biotin-labeled dCTP and dCAP, dTTP, and dGTP by Klenow and incubated at 37 °C for 30 min. The proximal chromatin DNA ligation was conducted using T4 DNA ligation enzyme at room temperature for 4 h. After centrifugation at 2500× <i>g</i> for 5 min, the reaction mixture was resuspended in SDS buffer (50 mM Tris-HCl, 1% SDS, 10 mM EDTA, pH 8.0), proteinase K was added, and the mixture was incubated at 55 °C for 30 min. The formaldehyde crosslinking of the nuclear complexes was reversed by the addition of 30 μl of 5 M NaCl and incubation at 65 °C overnight. For subsequent chromatin, DNA was purified and fragmented by sonication on a Covaris sonicator. After DNA repair and 3′ A addition, adaptor was added. The amplification of library molecules was performed according to the standard Illumina library preparation protocol. The libraries were sequenced on HiSeq X Ten DNA sequencers to obtain paired-end 150-nucleotide reads, following the manufacturer instructions (Illumina). The libraries were sequenced on HiSeq X Ten DNA sequencers to obtain paired-end 150-nucleotide reads, following the manufacturer instructions (Illumina). Two libraries were produced in this study, and each library produced about 400 million Hi-C reads. The high-quality reads were mapped to the draft scaffolds using a fast and accurate short-read alignment using a Burrows–Wheeler transform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Inference of human population history from individual whole-genome sequences. Nature 475, 493–496 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2680">48</a></sup>, and then the duplicated mapping reads and unmapped reads were removed using SAMtools (Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2684">49</a></sup>. The separations of HiC read pairs mapped in draft scaffolds were analyzed using chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions (LACHESIS) to produce a likelihood model for genomic distance between read pairs, and the model was used to identify putative misjoins. The greater the number of reads of interaction between two contigs, the greater the likelihood of a class. Contig clustering was done according to the number of interactions reads. Then, HiC data were used to do scaffolding using LACHESIS software, and finally about 94.56% sequences were grouped into 12 super scaffolds (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">3</a>). The contigs were sorted according to the intensity of every two contig interactions and the mapping location of interaction reads. The data on the clustering of the chromosomes and the assembly of <i>L. cubeba</i> are given in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Transposable elements and repetitive DNA</h3><p>TEs contribute to genome dynamism both in size and in structure, through insertion and eventual loss<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Hawkins, J. S., Proulx, S. R., Rapp, R. A. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Rapid DNA loss as a counterbalance to genome expansion through retrotransposon proliferation in plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 17811–17816 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2709">50</a></sup>. Genomic scaffolds were masked using RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) on the default settings after employing RepeatModeler/RepeatScout/Piler/LTR_finder software with RepBase database prediction. Meanwhile, the TEs in the genome were identified through Repeat ProteinMask soft annotation, using the RepBase database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Predictions of genes and noncoding RNA</h3><p>To generate gene models with high confidence, we applied a gene-annotation framework by combining evidence drawn from spliced transcripts of RNA-seq ab initio gene predictors and protein evidence drawn from orthologous proteins of closely related and model plant species. The detailed procedure was referred to Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">13</a> and finally 31,329 protein-coding genes were predicted (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a>). We then applied the functional assignments of protein-coding sequences of <i>L. cubeba</i> genes to the public protein databases KEGG (<a href="http://www.genome.jp/kegg/">http://www.genome.jp/kegg/</a>), SwissProt (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>), TrEMBL (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>), and InterProScan v5.11-51.0 (<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro/">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro/</a>). In this way, we generated functional assignments for 94.6% (29,651/31,329) of the available <i>L. cubeba</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">10</a>).</p><p>Noncoding RNA was determined using structural features and homology assignments. rRNA was determined via BLAST to rRNA sequencing of other species, using the high levels of conservation in species. tRNA was identified using tRNAscn-SE (<a href="http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/">http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/</a>). In addition, other types of noncoding RNA, including miRNA and snRNA, were identified using INFERNAL to search the Pfam database (<a href="http://infernal.janelia.org/">http://infernal.janelia.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Ortholog detection with OrthoMCL</h3><p>We downloaded genome and annotation data for <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (GCA_003024255.1), <i>Anthoceros angustus (not published)</i>, <i>Aquilegia coerulea</i> (GCA_002738505.1), <i>Ananas comosus</i> (GCF_001540865.1), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (TAIR 10), <i>Asparagus officinalis</i> (GCF_001876935.1), <i>A. trichopoda</i> (V1.0), <i>Beta vulgaris</i> (V1.2.2), <i>C. kanehirae</i> (GCA_003546025.1), <i>Citrus sinensis</i> (GCF_000317415.1), <i>Coffea canephora</i> (V1.0), <i>Cucumis sativus</i> (GCF_000004075.2), <i>Daucus carota</i> (v2.0), <i>G. biloba</i> (2019-06-04), <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> (v2.1), <i>Glycine soja</i> (v1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kim, M. Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032–22037 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2827">51</a></sup>, <i>Helianthus annuus</i> (GCF_002127325.1), <i>L. chinense</i> (GCA_003013855.2)<i>, Musa acuminate</i> (V1.0), <i>Macleaya cordata</i> (GCA_002174775.1), <i>Nelumbo nucifera</i> (GCF_000365185.1), <i>Nymphaea colorata</i> (GCA_902499525.1), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (v7.0), <i>P. americana</i> (v2.0), <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> (GCF_000413155.1), <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i> (APLD00000000.1), <i>Prunus persica</i> (V2.1), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (V3), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (SL2.50), <i>Ricinus communis</i> (GCF_000151685.1), <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> (GCA_001981405.1), <i>Theobroma cacao</i>(V1.1), <i>V. vinifera</i> (V12X), and <i>Zea mays</i> (v.2.1). We removed genes with open-reading frames of &lt;200 bp and performed gene family clustering using OrthoMCL.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene family expansion and contraction</h3><p>We measured the expansion and contraction of orthologous gene families using the software CAFÉ 4.2 (<a href="https://github.com/hahnlab/CAFE">https://github.com/hahnlab/CAFE</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987–1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2900">52</a></sup>. On the basis of the maximum likelihood modeling of gene gain and loss, we analyzed gene families for signs of expansion or contraction (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">4c</a>) using genome data from 26 species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">11</a>). Our KEGG enrichment analyses were conducted for unique, significantly expanded, and constructed gene families in Magnoliids, Lauraceae, and <i>L. cubeba</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Phylogenetic reconstruction</h3><p>In order to identify more phylogeny-informative sites, we screened the genome data of 26 species for common conserved gene families (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">11</a>) by including the single-copy genes in at least 22 species, and two copies in the remaining 4 species. Thus, the total number for a single gene family was no more than 30. In species with two copies of a given gene family, we selected the gene with the best BLAST hits. Finally, we obtained 1201 common conserved gene families in 26 species. For the gene families that had undergone expansion and contraction and the divergent time estimation, we constructed a phylogenetic tree based on these 1201 common conserved gene families using MrBayes software with GTR+<i>Γ</i> model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Huelsenbeck, J. P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754–755 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2927">35</a></sup>.</p><p>For phylogeny reconstruction in angiosperms, we derived 160 common single-copy gene families from BUSCO database for 34 species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1</a>); the phylogenetic tree was constructed based on concatenated single-copy gene family alignment and coalescent-based approaches that incorporate individual gene tree. A concatenated phylogenomic tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1</a>) was constructed using MrBayes with GTR+<i>Γ</i> Model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Huelsenbeck, J. P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754–755 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2943">35</a></sup>. For coalescent-based approaches, we used ASTRAL to combine gene trees from 160 single-copy genes, and the <i>q</i> value was used to account for variation among gene trees owing to ILS. For gene tree estimation, both the nucleotides and amino acids were used to reconstruct the phylogenetic trees by RAxML v.8.1.17<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2951">53</a></sup>. GTRGAMMA and GAMMAJTT were set as estimation models for the nucleotide and amino acid tree, respectively.</p><p>The multi-locus bootstrapping and the built-in local posterior probabilities of ASTRAL were used to estimate branch support<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mirarab, S. et al. ASTRAL: genome-scale coalescent-based species tree estimation. Bioinformatics 30, i541–i548 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2958">27</a></sup> and to test for polytomies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Sayyari, E. &amp; Mirarab, S. Testing for polytomies in phylogenetic species trees using quartet frequencies. Genes 9, 132 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d65370e2962">54</a></sup>. This estimation, which was conducted with the built-in functionality of ASTRAL (version 4.11.2) by finding the average number of gene tree quartets defined around the branch (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1</a>), resulted in percentage of gene trees that agreed with each branch in the species tree. We use “ASTRAL topology” to refer to the tree inferred from 160 unbinned amino acid alignments in which branches with 33% or less support is contracted, which refers to the potential ILS.</p><p>Phylogenomic analysis in Lauraceae was conducted using both nuclear and plastid genome data. To conduct phylogeny analysis of Lauraceae using single-copy gene families, we generated and integrated Illumina-sequenced transcriptomic data for various tissues (including flower buds, flowers, leaves, stems, buds, and bark) of 23 species representing 16 genera, representing the main lineage of Lauraceae and <i>C. praecox</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">23</a>) and the Pacbio Iso-seq data for <i>C. filiformis</i> and <i>C. praecox</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">4</a>). We developed a phylogenetic tree through a concatenated sequence alignment of 275 single-copy gene families as the phylogeny of the angiosperm (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3a</a>). We also used ASTRAL5.6.3 to reconstruct the species tree based on a data set similar to the concatenated tree (275 single-copy tree), and the ILS was also identified by −q 8 argument. The 1st and 2nd codon positions and 3rd codon position were derived from the same data set of the species tree, and used to reconstruct the phylogenetic trees based on a similar method. To perform a phylogenetic analysis using plastid genome data, we assembled the plastid genomes from incorporated re-sequenced data of 27 species representing 19 genera of Lauraceae as well as the outgroup <i>C. praecox</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">24</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Identification of whole-genome duplications in Laurales</h3><p><i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub>-based age distributions for all paralogous genes (paranome) of genomes and transcriptomes in Magnoliids were constructed. Concisely, the paranome was built by identifying gene families with the mclblastline pipeline (v10-201) (micans.org/mcl)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Enright, A. J., Van Dongen, S. &amp; Ouzounis, C. A. An efficient algorithm for large-scale detection of protein families. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 1575–1584 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3016">55</a></sup> after performing all-against-all BLASTP search with an <i>E</i> value cutoff of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>. Each gene family was aligned using MUSCLE (v3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3025">56</a></sup>. Then the CODEML program in the PAML package (v4.4c)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3030">57</a></sup> was used to estimate <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> for all pairwise comparisons within a gene family. Gene families were further subdivided into subfamilies for which <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> values between members did not exceed 5. As a gene family of <i>n</i> members produces <i>n</i>(<i>n</i> − 1)/2 pairwise <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> estimates for <i>n</i> − 1 retained duplication event, we corrected for the redundancy of <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> values by first inferring a phylogenetic tree for each subfamily using PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307–321 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3071">58</a></sup> with the default settings. Then, for each duplication node in the resulting phylogenetic tree, all <i>m K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> estimates for a duplication between the two child clades were added to the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distribution with a weight of 1/<i>m</i>, so that the sum of the weights of all <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> estimates for a single duplication event was 1.</p><p>To identify synteny or collinear segments in the genome of <i>L. cubeba</i>, i-ADHoRe (v3.0) was used with the parameters level_2_only=FALSE, enabling the ability to detect highly degenerated collinear segments resulting from more ancient large-scale duplications (this is achieved by recursively building genomic profiles based on relatively recent collinear segments)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Proost, S. et al. i-ADHoRe 3.0-fast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e11 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3103">59</a></sup>. The <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distribution of paralogs located on collinear segments (anchor pairs) was calculated using maximum likelihood in the CODEML program of the PAML package (v4.4c)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3113">57</a></sup>.</p><p>The <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub>-based ortholog age distributions were constructed by identifying one-to-one orthologs between species by selecting reciprocal best hits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Moreno-Hagelsieb, G. &amp; Latimer, K. Choosing BLAST options for better detection of orthologs as reciprocal best hits. Bioinformatics 24, 319–324 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3126">60</a></sup>, followed by <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> estimation using the CODEML program, as above. To compare different substitution rates in Magnoliids species, we compared the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distribution of one-to-one orthologs identified between <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>L. cubeba</i> and the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions of one-to-one orthologs identified between <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>C. kanehirae</i>, <i>P. americana</i>, <i>D. hainanensis</i>, <i>Nothaphoebe cavaleriei</i>, <i>C. filiformis</i>, <i>P. boldus</i>, <i>G. americanus</i>, <i>L. sempervirens</i>, <i>G. keule</i>, <i>C. praecox</i>, <i>I. australiense</i>, and <i>L. chinense</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a>). Because <i>V. vinifera</i> and Magnoliids diverged at a specific time, we would expect similar peaks in orthologous <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions if all Magnoliid species had similar substitution rates.</p><p>To circumscribe the placements of the WGDs identified in the genome of <i>L. cubeba</i> in the phylogeny of Magnoliids, we compared the anchor-pair <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distribution of <i>L. cubeba</i> and the orthologous <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions between <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>D. hainanensis</i>, <i>G. keule</i>, <i>C. filiformis</i>, <i>C. praecox</i>, <i>L. chinense</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i>. To quantify the differences in substitution rates among these Magnoliids species, we performed a relative rate test, using <i>V. vinifera</i> as an outgroup to calculate <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distances after the divergence between <i>L. cubeba</i> and each of the Magnoliids species. The <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distance between any two species in a relative rate test was estimated by the mode of their orthologous <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distribution. As the substitution rates seemed to vary considerably among the sequenced Magnoliids so far (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">8</a>), the calculated <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distance for <i>L. cubeba</i> in each relative rate test was used to correct the orthologous <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peaks between <i>L. cubeba</i> and other Magnoliids species under the assumption that the two species have an identical substitution rate after their divergence (arrows in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2b</a>). For example, using the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distance between <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>G. keule</i>, the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distance between <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>G. keule</i>, and the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distance between <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>L. cubeba</i>, we used a relative rate test to calculate <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distances to the lineage of <i>G. keule</i> and <i>L. cubeba</i> after their divergence, respectively. Then, orthologous <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> between <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>G. keule</i> was corrected by twice of the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distance to <i>L. cubeba</i> (assuming that <i>L. cubeba</i> and <i>G. keule</i> had the same substitution rate).</p><p>To further place the WGD peaks identified in the paranome <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distributions from other genomes and transcriptomes in Magnoliids (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">8</a>), we built gene families with a collection of species in Laurales and <i>L. chinense</i>, along with <i>A. trichopoda</i> and <i>G. biloba</i> as extra outgroups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2d</a>), using OrthoMCL on the default settings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Fischer, S. et al. Using OrthoMCL to assign proteins to OrthoMCL-DB groups or to cluster proteomes into new ortholog groups. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 35, 6.12.1–6.12.19 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3408">61</a></sup>. Among the identified gene families, we selected 23 single-copy gene families to estimate the branch lengths in the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> unit using PAML (v4.4c)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3418">57</a></sup> with the free-ratio model. The topology and absolute divergence times of the species tree were retrieved from TimeTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Suleski, M. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree: a resource for timelines, timetrees, and divergence times. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1812–1819 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3422">30</a></sup>. To infer the ages of WGDs in the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> unit as well, <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peaks were identified in a paranome <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distribution by an R function from github.com/stas-g/findPeaks after a smooth spline was fitted to the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> distribution. To obtain a 95% CI for each identified <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peak, <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> values of paralogs in a wide range of the estimated peak were resampled 100 times to obtain 100 bootstrapped peaks. To map all the identified <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> peaks and their 95% CIs onto the species phylogeny in the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> unit, we divided <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> values of the identified peaks and the 95% CIs by two, with the assumption that duplicate genes evolved at similar substitution rates after WGD events. We then considered each tip in the species phylogeny as a starting point and mapped half of the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> value of each peak from the tip toward the root of the phylogeny to date when WGD events occurred in the phylogeny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig2">2d</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Low-coverage genome sequencing and plastid genome assembly</h3><p>Low-coverage genome sequence data were generated for 47 species, including a 15× strategy for species in <i>Litsea</i> and a 30× strategy for species in other genera in Lauraceae (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">24</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">25</a>). The plastid genome data were de novo assembled. We also downloaded the complete plastid genomes of Lauraceae species from the NCBI and combined them into a single database. Then, BLAST was used to search against our plastid database, and the blast-hit pair reads were corresponding to the plastid origin, which accounted for about 3% for all species in this study. Then the PLATANUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 24, 1384–1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3509">62</a></sup> software was used to assemble the picked reads. After the contig was assembled, the scaffold tool from PLATANUS was used to scaffold the first assembly version based on the same paired-end reads to obtain the second assembly. Next, gap-closing was performed using the PLATANUS assembler to close the gap in the second assembly and obtain the final assembly, which contained at least three scaffolds, representing LSC, SSCm, and IRs. The scaffold parts were then annotated in DOGMA (<a href="http://dogma.ccbb.utexas.edu/">http://dogma.ccbb.utexas.edu/</a>), and artificially assembled into the almost complete plastid genome, with reference to the published plastid genome of Lauraceae. To identify the conservative segments for phylogenetic reconstruction, we used a HomBlocks pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Bi, G., Mao, Y., Xing, Q. &amp; Cao, M. HomBlocks: a multiple-alignment construction pipeline for organelle phylogenomics based on locally collinear block searching. Genomics 110, 18–22 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3518">63</a></sup> to locate the collinear regions for alignment. The plastid genomes were used to construct a phylogenetic tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Transcriptomic data and analysis in Lauraceae</h3><p>For library construction, a total of 1.5 µg RNA was prepared, and libraries were generated using the NEBNext<sup>®</sup> Ultra™ RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina<sup>®</sup> (NEB, USA). The index codes were put into attribute sequences for each sample. The clustering of the index-coded samples was carried out on a cBot Cluster Generation System using the TruSeq PE Cluster Kit v3-cBot-HS (Illumina). Subsequently, the libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq platform to produce paired-end reads. The raw reads were further processed using in-house Perl scripts to remove reads containing adapters, reads containing ploy-N, and low-quality reads. At the same time, Q20, Q30, and GC-content levels and sequence duplication levels of the clean data were calculated. All downstream analyses were conducted with high-quality clean data. Transcriptome assembly was accomplished using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3538">64</a></sup>, with min_kmer_cov set to 2 as a default and with all other parameters set to default values. All transcriptome assemblies were further valued by a BUSCO assessment (<a href="https://busco.ezlab.org/">https://busco.ezlab.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3546">23</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">20</a>). Protein sequences and coding sequences of the transcripts were predicted using TransDecoder (<a href="http://transdecoder.github.io">http://transdecoder.github.io</a>). For genes with more than one transcript, the longest was taken as the unigene and gene expression levels were estimated by RSEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinforma. 12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3558">65</a></sup>. Gene (or transcript isoform) expression values were provided using the fragments per kilobase per million mapped reads method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Mortazavi, A., Williams, B. A., McCue, K., Schaeffer, L. &amp; Wold, B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat. Methods 5, 621–628 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3562">66</a></sup>. To determine whether the chimeric transcripts occurred in the gene family of TPSs, we conducted a local BLAST (<i>E</i> value &lt;1 × 10<sup>−6</sup>) and found that 18 transcripts from the de novo assembled transcriptome of <i>L. cubeba</i> were corresponded to the genome data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">35</a>).</p><p>We generated three sets of transcriptome data. First, we obtained Illumina-sequenced transcriptomic data for various <i>L. cubeba</i> tissues to enable the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome assembly. The transcriptome data were mapped on to the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome for gene expression analysis. Second, we generated and integrated Illumina-sequenced transcriptomic data for various tissues (including flower buds, flowers, leaves, stems, buds, and bark) of 23 species in 16 genera, representing the main lineage of Lauraceae and <i>C. praecox</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">23</a>), and the Pacbio Iso-seq data of <i>C. filiformis</i> and <i>C. praecox</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">4</a>). These de novo mixed-tissue transcriptome data were used to conduct a phylogenetic analysis of Lauraceae, and were also used to annotate gene homologs, including the <i>TPSs</i> and <i>DXSs</i> of this family. The gene homologs were identified using the HMMER software package. Third, to explore the genes involved in the regulation of the evolution of inflorescences of Lauraceae, we generated transcriptomic data for flower buds in triplicate for 21 species, representing 13 genera in Lauraceae (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">26</a>).</p><p>The transcriptomes of flower buds were used to excavate the genes involved in inflorescence adaptation and sexual differentiation in Lauraceae. We screened genes that had been reported to be involved in panicle and perianth development in other species. The phylogenetic trees of the candidate genes in Lauraceae were constructed. Only the phylogenetic tree constructed by the FUWA homologs of Lauraceae was consistent with the evolutionary characteristics of inflorescences in this family. The detailed method for the selection of FUWA in Lauraceae is given in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">8</a>. The expression levels of the gene homologs in Lauraceae were compared and <i>PTL</i> was found to have a similar expression pattern to that during the presentation of the abscission of the perianth tube and its encapsulation in fruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig3">3f</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">9</a>). Moreover, the DEGs involved in the development of bisexual and unisexual flower buds of Lauraceae were analyzed based on the flower bud transcriptome data. The DEGs (fold change &gt;2, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) between the female and male flowers in <i>Litsea tsinlingensis</i>, <i>Litsea rubescens</i>, <i>L. cubeba</i>, <i>Lindera megaphylla</i>, and <i>Sassafras tzumu</i>, were selected. KEGG pathway and GO term-enrichment analyses of DEGs were subsequently conducted for each species. Interestingly, the DEGs were observed to be significantly enriched in the Plant Hormone Signal Transduction (map04075) in each species. Unexpectedly, TAG10 and a hypothesized protein (Lcu01G_02292 in the region of 124099255-124107806 in chr1 of the <i>L. cubeba</i> genome) were included in the enriched plant hormone signal transduction pathway of each of the above species, and exhibited distinctively different expression modes between male and female flower buds. Finally, we analyzed the expression modes of <i>TAG10</i> and the hypothetical protein in the transcriptome data of male, female, and bisexual flower buds in Lauraceae. The detailed method for the selection of <i>PTL</i>, <i>FUWA</i>, and <i>TGA10</i> genes in Lauraceae are given in Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">8</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">10</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">TPSs identification and functional validation experiments</h3><p>To avoid missing potential TPS genes, candidate TPSs were identified from the predicted proteomes of <i>L. cubeba</i> and other species by pfamscan based on the HMMER suite (<a href="http://hmmer.janelia.org/">http://hmmer.janelia.org/</a>), using the Pfam profiles of PF01397 and PF03936 as queries (<i>E</i> value &lt; 10<sup>−5</sup>) with a protein length over 200 amino acids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 66, 212–229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3698">9</a></sup>. The candidate TPS genes were further inspected manually using InterProScan5 (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro/">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro/</a>) to confirm putative full-length TPS genes. A total of 52 such full-length <i>LcuTPS</i> genes were identified. Although the above criteria may result in the identification of pseudo- or partial genes, 41 of the 52 identified TPS genes were found to have more than 500 amino acids. The full-length TPSs were analyzed with ChloroP for the prediction of N-terminal plastidial targeting peptides (<a href="http://www.cbs.dtu.dk/services/ChloroP/">http://www.cbs.dtu.dk/services/ChloroP/</a>). The analysis of the exon/intron structures of the full-length <i>TPS</i> genes were also conducted using GSDS (<a href="http://gsds.cbi.pku.edu.cn/">http://gsds.cbi.pku.edu.cn/</a>), and the conserved motif RR(X)<sub>8</sub>W and DDXXD were labeled (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">21</a>). The MG2C (<a href="http://mg2c.iask.in/mg2c_v2.0/">http://mg2c.iask.in/mg2c_v2.0/</a>) software was used to construct gene distribution maps of <i>L. cubeba</i> chromosomes. Putative full-length TPSs (&gt;200 amino acids in length) identified in <i>L. cubeba</i> and other sequenced plant genomes (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">33</a>) and maximum likelihood trees were built using CIPRES (<a href="https://www.phylo.org">https://www.phylo.org</a>) with the JTT model using 1000 bootstrap replicates.</p><p>Gene function was validated in vivo and in vitro. For gene function validation in vivo, endogenous transient overexpression was performed in <i>L. cubeba</i> and tobacco (<i>N. benthamiana</i>) leaves. The empty vector and constructs containing <i>LcuTPS19</i>, <i>LcuTPS20</i>, <i>LcuTPS22</i>, <i>LcuTPS25</i>, and <i>LcuTPS42</i> were carried by <i>Agrobacterium</i> cultures and infiltrated into the leaves using a 1 mL needleless syringe. After infiltration, the plants were grown for 2 days; then, a leaf near (&lt;5 mm) the infiltration point was collected and immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen. These samples were stored at −80 °C for volatile analysis using GC-MS, with 1 μg of ethyl decanoate added to serve as an internal standard. For GC-MS analysis, the samples were ground and incubated at 40 °C for 30 min. The volatiles were further extracted using SPME fiber with 50/30 μm divinylbenzene/carboxen/polydimethylsiloxane (DVB/CAR/PDMS) (Supelco Co., Bellefonte, PA, USA). GC-MS analysis was conducted on an Agilent 6890N gas chromatograph coupled to a mass spectrometer (Agilent 5975B, Santa Clara, CA, USA) with a fused silica capillary column (DB-5MS) coated with polydimethylsiloxane (19091 S-433) (60 m × 0.25 mm internal diameter, 0.25 μm film thickness). The oven temperature was programmed to start at 50 °C for 2 min, and then ramped to 80 °C at a rate of 3 °C min<sup>−1</sup>, followed by a second ramp to 180 °C at a rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>, and a third ramp to 230 °C at a rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup>, finally, ramp to 250 °C at a rate of 20 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. The conditions were as follows: ion source, 230 °C; electron energy, 70 eV; GC-MS interface zone, 250 °C, and a scan range of 50–500 m/z. There were three biological replicates for transient overexpression analysis. To identify the target monoterpene, the retention time was compared with that of an authentic standard purchased from Sigma-Aldrich, which was further validated using the NIST Mass Spectral Library. The primers are shown in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">36</a>. The details of the experimental procedure for transient expression analysis in <i>L. cubeba</i> and tobacco are given in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">14</a>.</p><p>For gene function validation in vitro, the full-length open-reading frames of <i>LcuTPS22</i>, <i>LcuTPS25</i>, and <i>LcuTPS42</i> were cloned and inserted into the pET28a vector, and then transformed into <i>E. coli</i> BL21 (DE3) pLysS cells (Transgen, China). Recombinant protein was induced with 0.2 mM isopropyl-β-d-galactopyranoside for 20 h at 16 °C, and the expressed recombinant protein was then purified. In the enzymatic assays, the recombinant protein was incubated with 25 mM HEPES, pH 7.2, 100 mM KCl, 10 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>, 10% (v/v) glycerol, 5 mM DTT, and 30 μM geranyl diphosphate (GPP, Sigma) in pH 7.2 at 30 °C for 1 h<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Chang, Y. T. &amp; Chu, F. H. Molecular cloning and characterization of monoterpene synthases from Litsea cubeba (Lour.) Persoon. Tree Genet. Genomes 7, 835–844 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d65370e3812">24</a></sup>. The volatiles were analyzed using GC-MS analysis. To identify the target monoterpene, the retention time was compared with that of an authentic standard purchased from Sigma-Aldrich, which was further validated using the NIST Mass Spectral Library. There were three biological replicates for the analysis of the enzyme activity. The primers are shown in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">36</a>, and the details of the procedure are given in the Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM1">14</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>A reporting summary for this article is available as a Supplementary Information file. Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. The data sets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The genome and transcriptome sequences described in this manuscript have been submitted to the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) under accession codes <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA562049">PRJNA562049</a> [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA562049">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA562049</a>] (whole genome and assembly data), <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA562115">PRJNA562115</a> [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA562115">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA562115</a>] (transcriptome data of 23 Lauraceae species), and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA562080">PRJNA562080</a> [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA562080">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA562080</a>] (low-coverage genome data of 47 Lauraceae species). The data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-15493-5#Fig1">1a, c</a>, <a data-track="click"
